1. WELCOME AND CONFLICTS OF INTEREST

1.1 The Chair welcomed PwC to the meeting and especially Chris Neale who was attending his first
meeting and would be joining two ARC meetings a year to provide an additional perspective,
particularly on IT change.

1.2 The Directors declared that they had no conflicts of interest in the matters to be considered at
the meeting in accordance with the requirements of section 177 of the Companies Act 2006 and
the Company’s Articles of Association.

2. UPDATE FROM SUBSIDIARIES.

Tim Franklin provided an overview of the key issues from recent PO Insurance ARC meetings:

PO Insurance had commissioned a review of TIF, its travel insurance underwriter, following
reports alleging instances of poor treatment of customers. The initial findings of the review
were being analysed but the PO Insurance Board wished to look at alternative providers

‘the Competition and Markets Authority’s (CMA) response to the super complaint lodged by
Citizens Advice created a less stable environment for both PO Insurance and Telecoms. It
was hard to gauge what the industry’s response would need to be but we were monitoring
the situation.

It was AGREED that the CMA’s response to the super complaint should be shown as a potential
strategic risk on the risk register. PO Limited had a significant volume of vulnerable customers
and customers who were over 65 and these groups were a focus for the regulator. It was
confirmed that we had information on the pricing differentials for Telco.

It was noted that we remained 20% out of tolerance for the quality of sales of financial services
products in the branch network. The situation had been notably problematic with some of the
multiples like WH Smiths. It was asked whether we should we be taking a different approach in
light of this and a request was made for ARC to look at the issue in more depth. It was also
suggested that we needed a marginal contribution analysis for the multiples and it was reported

SECURITY STRATEGY

Rob Houghton and Mick Mitchell joined the meeting. RH reported that a more comprehensive

Security strategy report was being provided to the Committee following the discussions at the

Board meeting in November 2018. The next steps were to carry out a deep dive with Deloitte,

following which it was suggested we provide quarterly reports to the ARC showing how we were RH/MM
performing against the metrics agreed.

The strategy focussed on:

the human element of weak passwords, susceptibility to phishing and so forth. There was
an enhanced communications plan and a tool was being implemented to measure password
strength. We were communicating with staff about these issues as well as testing password
strength. We were making clear to people their personal exposure as well as their
professional exposure where basic security requirements were not adhered to. JH would
include regular reports on progress. This would be ongoing activity, not a programme of
communications and training for a period of time

. third party suppliers and their security arrangements

¢ improving detection and incident responses

* mandatory training for new joiners as well as annual mandatory training for all staff,
recognising that additional reinforcement was needed

INTERNAL AUDIT REPORT
Johann Appel introduced the report and highlighted the key issues:

* the Internal Audit Team were now fully staffed and Deloitte had been on-boarded as
Internal Audit co-partner. Five audits had been completed since October 2018 and eight
were in flight. The relationship with Deloitte was developing well and their day-to-day
management was good. We were meeting regularly with senior people at Deloitte

e —adraft Internal Audit Plan for 2019-20 would be brought to the March ARC a more holistic
approach had been taken to some of the audits we had been carrying out in Chesterfield
and this was proving useful.

Anumber of points were raised, including:

* whether we had an agreed timeline for responding to Internal Audit reports and why it had
taken so long for the Expenses Internal Audit report to be issued? AC reported that he had
not been comfortable that enough work had been done to see whether we had any fraud risk
before issuing the report, especially in relation to corporate credit cards. JA reported that the
team had identified a significant number of exceptions to the policy that had needed to be
investigated with receipts requested from credit card holders as well as testing a sample of
employee expenses. It was noted that the team had looked at a large data set and had
followed up the most significant exceptions. KM noted that he would rather that the
Committee receive an initial findings report, setting out the further work which needed to be
done and follow this up with a stage 2 investigation. Such issues and delays needed to be
flagged to the Chair of ARC

« when had we had last reviewed the availability of corporate credit cards? It was reported that
the recent review had led to the number of corporate credit cards being reduced from 96 to
35. We were in the process of on-boarding a new supplier which had better reporting
capability. The expenses policy had been revised to align better with business requirements.
The Selenity expenses system had been in place for a year and we had agreed to do some re-
training for managers not only on checking the validity of expense claims but also ensuring
that these were coded correctly. Committee Members noted that training was vital and
individuals needed to be alerted when they were not adhering to the policy. There also had
to be an onus on managers to check claims and the receipts. JA reported that spot checks
would continue and a follow-up report would be undertaken in about six months.

AUDIT STRATEGY MEMORANDUM

Andrew Paynter introduced the Audit Strategy Memorandum. The meeting with EY had not
taken place in October 2018 as planned originally but had now been held and a detailed file
review undertaken.

PwC was hoping to be able to rely on the controls within Horizon. PO Limited was a complex
business and significant systems change work had been taking place. PwC’s approach reflected
that this was a higher risk audit for them. PwC would being providing the same level of
information it would for a listed company even though this was not a requirement for PO
Limited.

EY had finished their final audit reports for PO Limited and these had been signed off; the only
outstanding item was their formal resignation letter [post-meeting note: EY’s formal resignation
letter was received on 13.02.2019].

PwC’s engagement letter had been agreed verbally. The Chair of ARC would be advised once all
the appropriate paperwork had been signed off.

The significant risks for PwC were:

Risk of Management override of control?

Fraud in revenue recognition?

Impairment of intangible assets subject to amortisation
« Impairment of fixed assets.

Postmaster litigation, would continue to be a focus and updates were received from Jane
MacLeod.

The significant risks were prescribed by auditing standards and included data; ledgers with higher
risk characteristics; any significant one-off transactions (e.g. the acquisition of Payzone Bill
Payments); fraudulent revenue recognition.

Increased risk would be signalled by manual intervention requirements and particular attention
would be given to those areas. IRS16, the new leasing accounting standard, had been issued. PO
Limited had a high number of leases so this would have a material impact. Investment spending
would also be a key focus.

The report set out the approach taken to financial processes in 2017/18 and how PwC would
take this forward for 2018/19. PwC was seeking to bring the Horizon system into scope. PwC
was finalising its work on the operation of the controls. The report set out PWC’s proposed audit
approach in 2019/20 as it transitioned from EY.

Anumber of comments were raised, including:

that we had to make sure we were aware of any other work PwC was doing for PO Limited
and that appropriate safeguards were in place

this was a focus of activity for us. CS suggested that the audit tests proceeded as planned but
that if a problem was identified this should be flagged and consideration given to re-
categorising the risk

why Successfactors remained out of scope for 2019/20? PwC reported that this would be an
extensive piece of work but an issue they would continue to revisit. AC noted that we were
starting to look at Successfactors’ long-term strategic position, including considering whether

POST OFFICE LIMITED PAGE 1 OF 5
AUDIT, RISK & COMPLIANCE COMMITTEE PROJECT UPDATE

EUM Programme update

Author: Julie Thomas Programme Manager: Esther Harvey

Executive Summary

Context

The Enhanced User Management (EUM) Programme was first started in October 2016
in the FS & T area to ensure branches could continue to trade Financial Services
products. A Smart ID would be issued to Horizon users only once they had passed
vetting, and this was to be linked to their Success Factors learning records to prevent
them transacting any product for which they were not fully trained. The solution would
additionally cover all products where mandatary training was required to cover Post
Offices regulatory obligations across a range of areas.

The original case expected all c.60,000 Horizon users to provide an individual email
address and that they would use Success Factors to complete their training.

In August 2017, the programme had stalled due to operators and their assistants
proving unwilling to supply individual email addresses or use Success Factors for their
training. Historically, branches had been used to using Horizon for all training.
Following a short review, a revised design and new business case was developed and
approved by POL Board in January 2018.

This paper gives an update vs the revised case which, although represented a 12 month
delay to roll-out and a £2.7m overspend to the original case, gave a solution which has
succeeded in fully delivering the original desired outcome whilst minimising operational
impact in our branches and for operators.

As the programme moves towards closure, a post implementation review has been
instigated by the risk team to capture the key lessons learned during the entire duration
of this programme.

Questions this paper addresses

1. What is the status and outlook?
2. What are the next steps?

Conclusion
1. The EUM programme remains on track for roll-out with 11,400 branches now live.

7,500 branches are now under training controls and demonstrating c.90%
conformance with minimal reminder activity required. The rest of the network is

planned to enter full training controls by the end of February 2019 in time for the

2. There are a number of remaining issues currently being worked through, including

vetting is being pursued, but these issues are not expected to impact the
programme’s ability to deliver the full roll-out within budget.

POST OFFICE PAGE 3 OF 5

The Report

What are we trying to achieve?

1.

The EUM programme will enable Post Office to be confident that all Horizon users
have been appropriately vetted, are up to date with mandatory training
requirements, and we will be able to demonstrate this to our regulators across
mainly Mails and Financial Services products.

. In order to achieve this, a combination of EUM and Common Services Programme

has collated detailed user data for every one of Post Office’s c.60,000 front line
colleagues. This data is stored in the SuccessFactors database, supported by SAP.
The data holds verification of each user’s vetting record and is accessed by Success
Factors LMS and Forgerock to enable a Horizon user’s Smart ID to govern the
products they are able to transact on Horizon.

. A significant barrier to overcome in the new design was the operational need for

single operators to use 2 or more Horizon terminals so that they can process Drop
and Go mail, serve Travel customers or prepare Mails despatches in between serving
other customers. To achieve this, a ‘suspend’ function was introduced so that,
although Horizon users can only use one terminal at a time, they don’t need to
complete the transaction in one basket to be able to start serving in the second.

. Another requirement was to allow users to continue to use Horizon for their

mandatory training, but to also give an option for them to use Success Factors since
the long term goal will be to deliver all training via a purpose built LMS system. The
design with SuccessFactors learning acting as the master for LMS and Horizon
compliance data has allowed this flexibility.

What is the status and outlook?

Is the project on time?

5.

The programme had been extended by 12 months, but is expected to complete roll-
out to the entire fixed network by 28 February 2019, this is in line with the revised
business case submitted in January 2018.

. The main constraint on roll-out speed has been the Common Services Programme’s

ability to collect user data. Branches typically don’t respond first time to requests
for data and then the data provided is often incomplete. Contributory factors include
absentee Postmasters where the Manager in charge does not wish to get involved in
dealing with Assistant’s personal data, and a large element of Postmasters who have
simply not provided data because they did not wish to accept the controls this
system introduces.

. Close working with the NFSP has resulted in a greater acceptance for the change

and this has been supported by a comprehensive communication plan which explains
the benefits to operators and customers, in particular, Post Office’s ability to win and
retain new business as a result of this change.

There are c. 800 users in 180 branches yet to provide data to allow us to issue Smart
IDs. These branches are currently being pursued in using an increasingly hardline

approach, starting with targeted communications, followed by a field team visit to
assist the switch, and eventually the old Horizon ID will be disabled. This approach
has been used so far on 700 branches with 95% success rate . Whilst this is

9. There is a current Change Request being progressed within Fujitsu which will prevent
users logged on to two terminals from completing final balancing operations in one
terminal while a second terminal is still in use. This has happened on 21 occasions
over 6 months and is being closely managed by Fujitsu. As soon as an instance is
reported, the branch is contacted to explain the balancing mis-match issues they
may create and any issue is immediately corrected by the Transaction Correction
team. A network wide communication was sent in early January. The change is
expected to become effective from May / June 2019 and will not exceed the
programme budget or require an extension of the programme team.

11.The key outcome of the project is to ensure POL is compliant with various

out of Horizon through training controls if they fail to complete training on time.

12.The latest training conformance rating was over 90% for SmartID users selling FS
products. This performance has been achieved with minimal intervention by Branch

end of March 19 as the programme closes.

13.Training controls are already in place for 7,500 users, and the rest of the network
will be activated in February 2019, meaning all branches will be under training
controls for future mandatory training modules from then on.

AUDIT, RISK & COMPLIANCE COMMITTEE

Financial Crime Risk Update

Author: Sally Smith Sponsor: Ian Holloway Meeting date: 20 September 2018

Executive Summary

Context

Post Office Limited provides second line financial crime services to POMS under the
Master Services Agreement. This paper updates the Audit and Risk Committee on
financial crime risks relating to Anti-Money Laundering (AML) and Counter Terrorist
Financing (CTF), and incorporates the Money Laundering Reporting Officer’s (MLRO)
annual report on compliance with The Money Laundering, Terrorist Financing and
Transfer of Funds (Information on the Payer) Regulations 2017 and The Terrorism Act
2000.

Questions this paper addresses

e What are the key AML and CTF risks within Post Office Managed Services Insurance
products and are there any significant gaps or weaknesses in compliance with its
regulatory obligations under the Money Laundering Regulations (MLRs)?

e Are the minimum control standards in the Post Office’s Anti-Bribery and Corruption
Policy being effectively applied?

Conclusion

1. Money laundering risks within the Post Office Management Services business are
relatively low, and generally there are adequate controls in place to mitigate any
AML risks that the business is exposed to.

2. Policies and procedures, monitoring and controls to mitigate Post Office
Management Services exposure to financial crime are in place and communicated
effectively to Third Parties.

3. Financial Crime Risk Assessments undertaken in May 2017 concluded that
insurance products within Post Office Management Services remit were within the
risk appetite set by the business. Although there have been no regulatory
changes, and no issues or incidents reported or identified, these require annual
re-assessment to ensure that this position remains.

4. We have not identified any instances indicative of Bribery or Corruption or failure
to report and approve gifts and hospitality.

Recommendations for Action

5. Product managers must ensure that there is an up to date product information
pack, which includes the treatment of PEPs and Sanctions checks to ensure that
financial crime risk assessments are maintained.

Strictly Confidential Financial Crime Risk Update

Post Office Limited - Audit Risk & Compliance Committee-25/03/19 17 of 176

6. POMS holds a detailed question set as a template for annual attestations for third
parties to complete. At present, there are instances where the questions are not
answered by third parties to evidence controls. POMS should ensure that the
annual attestations are completed in their entirety by third party providers.

Input Sought

The Committee is asked to note the contents the MLRO report and endorse the
recommendations.

am

A. Purpose and Scope of Report

1. The Money Laundering Regulations (MLRs) require that the Money Laundering
Reporting Officer (MLRO) produces an annual report to appraise senior
management on the effectiveness of key Anti-Money Laundering and Counter
Terrorist Financing (AML/CTF) controls, and make appropriate recommendations
for improvement in the management of risks and priorities, including resources
where appropriate.

2. The MLRs and the 2017 National Risk Assessment clearly identify a requirement
for organisations to adopt a risk-based approach to prevent money laundering and
terrorist financing. Risk assessment must be documented and evidence the
decisions that senior management have made in the context of the particular risks
facing the business.

B. Background

Strictly Confidential Financial Crime Risk Update

Post Office Limited - Audit Risk & Compliance Committee-25/03/19 19 of 176
Tab 3.2.2 Appendix 2

POST OFFICE MANAGEMENT SERVICES LIMITED PAGE 4 OF 11

3. Post Office Limited provides second line financial crime services, including AML/CTF

oversight, to POMS under the Master Services Agreement.

4. General insurance products are not subject to the Money Laundering Regulations

but firms operating within this sector remain subject to the Proceeds of Crime Act
2002 and are required by the Financial Conduct Authority to have systems and
controls in place to prevent financial crime. Without these controls, firms are at
risk of committing money-laundering offences. Therefore, most insurance
businesses implement controls adopted by firms subject to the Money Laundering
Regulations and AML rules.

5. There are a number of products and services that are sold or serviced through

branches, the Internet and call centres which are provided on behalf of clients, or
white labelled as Post Office. Whilst these products are regulated by the Financial
Conduct Authority (FCA), the client or supplier is responsible for the regulatory
activity. Post Office Management Services is required to meet its contractual
obligations to them, but also to report any suspicious activity direct to the NCA, as
required

C. Governance - those responsible for anti-money laundering
systems and controls, and the structure within which they
operate

6. The POMS Head of Risk and Compliance is responsible for oversight of compliance

controls in place for POMS. The POMS Head of Risk and Compliance receives
assistance from the MLRO of its appointed representative Post Office Ltd and the
Financial Crime team to assess Post Office Management Services’ exposure to
financial crime.

7. Post Office Management Services has delegated regulatory training within POMS

to Post Office Ltd. The training in place includes advising on how to proceed once
an internal report and/or SAR has been made and the design and implementation
of internal anti-money laundering systems and procedures.

8. Post Office Management Services’ Appointed Representative, Post Office Limited,

has a wider product and service provision for which it is regulated. Thus, Post
Office Ltd is subject to further regulatory obligations. Following an interim
appointment, the Head of Financial Crime, Sally Smith, took over as MLRO in
September 2017. The MLRO is located in Finsbury Dials, Moorgate, London, where
Post Office Group is situated.

9. The MLRO and Financial Crime Team report regularly on financial crime matters to

the Post Office Risk & Compliance Committee (R&CC) and the Post Office Audit
and Risk Committee (ARC).

10. The MLRO is the focal point of all AML/CTF activities and with the assistance of the

20 of 176

Financial Crime team is responsible for assessing and assuring Post Office Limited's
exposure to financial crime. This responsibility includes setting policies and
standards relating to financial crime, assessing and assuring AML/CTF risks across
Post Office, the provision of training and awareness within Post Office, the design
and implementation of internal anti-money laundering systems and procedures,
and advising on how to proceed once an internal report and/or Suspicious Activity

D. Operation and Effectiveness of Control Framework
i. Senior management oversight
13. See Section C above for summary of governance and oversight.

ii. Staff awareness and training

14. Provision of staff awareness and training is a key control for Post Office. All back
office and customer facing staff are required to complete annual AML/CTF training:
« For back office staff this must be completed within 30 days of joining and
annually
« For customer facing staff this must be completed before they have access to
Horizon and annually

15. Annual training was rolled out in May 2018 to all back office and front office staff
and agents.

16. Weekly monitoring of mandatory training completion levels for back office staff is
provided to relevant HR Directors via Success Factors data for them to chase ,and
where necessary, line managers follow through via performance management with
individuals who have not completed training as required.

17. Directly Managed and Agency Branch Training is delivered via Horizon and
completion is currently tracked by the Branch Standards Team. Smart ID is due
to be rolled out to all branches by the beginning of 2019, and training controls are
due to be implemented from September 2018 for those branches that have already
migrated. These training controls will ensure that no customer facing staff will be
able to transact regulated products on Horizon unless they have completed
mandatory training.

18. An annual Training, Awareness and Communication plan is refreshed each year to
aid the delivery of regular branch and business awareness communications on
AML, Financial Crime and SAR reporting.

Risk assessment, policies, controls and procedures

Strictly Confidential Financial Crime Risk Update

Post Office Limited - Audit Risk & Compliance Committee-25/03/19 21 of 176

POST OFFICE MANAGEMENT SERVICES LIMITED PAGE 6 OF 11

19.

22.

23;

24.

25.

Post Office Managed Services have developed a comprehensive set of risk
appetites for their business, which includes ensuring that it develops appropriate
and balanced controls to mitigate its financial crime risk. In conjunction with POL
these controls include measures to combat fraud, bribery and corruption, money
laundering and controls to report effectively against sanctioned individuals. POMS
has no appetite for public censure, fine or other significant regulatory sanction
from any UK regulatory body arising from these financial crime risks.

Financial crime risk assessments are undertaken on all POMS products prior to
launch and re-assessed annually, unless there is a product change or a material
incident, in which case the risks are re-assessed at that time (see Annex A for a
summary of the current assessments). The Financial Crime Team have been
engaged to support risk assessment and risk mitigation for Project Nemesis (Motor
and Household Insurance).

Policies relating to Financial Crime overall and AML/CTF specifically, were updated
during 2017 and approved at the September 2017 Audit and Risk Committee.
These clearly set out the minimum control standards required and who in the
business is responsible for them, and are subject to annual review. Both first and
second line management have responsibility to ensure that the controls in place
work as intended.

The Financial Crime Team undertake assurance activity on the application across
the business of the minimum control standards as set out in the policies, reporting
any issues identified to the Post Office R&CC and ARC.

Additionally, the Financial Crime Committee, chaired by Jane MacLeod has been
established to oversight the principle risk of Financial Crime across Post Office and
is attended by the POMS Head of Risk and Compliance.

Claims handling poses a financial crime risk regarding fraudulent or false claims
which require monitoring. POMS is not directly regulated for claims handling
activities which are undertaken by other regulated intermediaries.

For Travel Insurance, claims handling is contractually outsourced by POMS to
Collinson’s and TIF, and for Life Insurance, claims handling is contractually
outsourced by POMS to Royal London. All claims relating to sales prior to 1%
January 2017 sit with the previous Life Insurance provider, Aviva. Regular reports
are provided to POMS concerning claims handling by the respective intermediaries
and POMS does not hold any direct regulatory responsibility for claims handling
relating to Travel and Life Insurance. The business does remain exposed to
reputational risk which is managed through the collation of management
information and root cause analysis.

iv. Development of new products

26.

There have been no new POMS products and services in the period under review.
However, a Business Readiness Assurance approval process exists which involves
multiple approval points that evaluate the confidence that the business has in
accepting the change into its operational environment and ensures relevant
AML/CTF risk assessment. The Financial Crime team ensure that all new or
amended products and services have been through the financial crime risk
assessment process prior to approval of Business Readiness.

. Customer due diligence (CDD)

30.

Post Office Management Services should be notified by the third party in

. Business areas

ii. Fifth Anti Money Laundering Directive

38. The Fifth Money Laundering Directive was announced on 30" November 2016 and

its principle aims appear to be tackling the finance of terrorism. On 20" December
2017, EU ambassadors confirmed that agreement had been reached between the
European Parliament and the Council regarding the latest amendments. These now
need to be endorsed by the European Parliament and Council and they will then
need to be transposed into national legislation within 18 months.

39. There are no significant impacts in respect of POMS products.

Criminal Finances Act

40. The Act came into force in September 2017, and a working group was established

by Legal with support from the Financial Crime team to ensure that a documented
risk assessment was produced and measures put in place to establish a defence
for Post Office. The primary risk relates to the criminal facilitation of criminal tax
evasion by a Post Office ‘associated person’, although this is currently deemed to
be low risk.

41. The working group have examined the reasonableness of procedures and controls

relating to postmasters, contractors, employees and suppliers, and actions have
been taken to address any areas of weakness.

iv. Sanctions and Anti-Money Laundering Bill

42. This bill was introduced on 18th October 2017, and it is designed to:

e enable the UK to continue to implement United Nations (UN) sanctions regimes
and to use sanctions to meet national security and foreign policy objectives
post-Brexit; and

e enable anti-money laundering and counter-terrorist financing measures to be
kept up to date, helping to protect the security and prosperity of the UK and
continue to align the UK with international standards

43. There are no significant impacts expected for POMS.

v. Joint Money Laundering Intelligence Taskforce (JMLIT)

44. Post Office continues to participate in an information sharing agreement with the

National Crime Agency. The Head of Financial Crime regularly attends JMLIT
meetings as a means of ensuring market intelligence and horizon scanning of
issues relevant to Post Office activity can be considered on a proactive basis.

45. The terrorist attacks in the UK in 2017 saw financial investigations being co-

ordinated via the JMLIT members, and c.15 requests for information were
responded to by members (including Post Office) in the hours after each event.

46. Prior to these attacks, the main terrorist finance red flags were to help identify

funding activity for overseas terror groups, funding the outward or inward journeys
of terrorist fighters, and the funding and activities of groups that preach or incite
racial hatred. c.40% of terrorist financing is financed by low level criminality, basic
fraud and robbery.

47. It is anticipated that going forward, security services will require the financial

more on domestic activity. In the 2017 attacks transactions were small, money
was obtained and used quickly, often in cash or through other instruments like

Conclusions and Recommendations

Money laundering risks within the POMS business are relatively low, and generally
there are adequate controls in place to mitigate any risks the business is exposed
to.

With the size and complexity of the Post Office Network, one of the key AML/CTF
controls for Post Office is robust training and awareness for all relevant staff. In
2017 there was an improvement in terms of corporate culture and individual
responsibility towards regulatory training and monitoring completion, and the
implementation of SmartID and training controls for Horizon users during 2018/19
will ensure that this is consistently applied across the business.

POMS have a responsibility to ensure that they support and promote effective
training and awareness across product managers and sales teams.

The financial crime risk assessment methodology used for POMS products in May
2017 has been built into a replacement online product and service risk assessment
tool for product managers to use for new products and services and this went live
in April 2018.

All product managers are required to document a detailed product information
pack, to inform this process, and POMS senior management should ensure that
these are completed fully and in a timely manner to enable up to date product
financial crime assessments to be maintained.

These product information packs should include the approach to PEPs and
Sanctions checking for that product and cover the responsibilities of both POMs
and third party suppliers in this regard.

Work has commenced with relevant product managers to produce product
information packs for all existing products that were assessed in May 2017 to assist
with the annual review process which is now due.

As highlighted in last year’s report, POMS should ensure that the annual
attestations are completed in their entirety by third party providers.

Appendix B

Anti-Bribery and Corruption Compliance

1.

The ABC Policy was reviewed and updated in July 2018, and has been signed off by
Post Office R&CC and ARC. The updated policy and a brief summary of the minor
amendments is included in papers for POMS ARC for September 2018.

The new Gifts and Hospitality reporting tool was launched in August 2017, and
reports are now provided to all Post Office GE members each quarter which
summarise the reports received for their area and highlight any issues identified.

Since the launch of the new tool, there have been no gifts or hospitality reported for
the Post Office Insurance employees. This is not inconsistent with the business
sector, and all employees are subject to the same training, awareness and
communications as Post Office employees.

Annual ABC training was delivered for completion on 21*t August 2018, and as at the
end of December completion rates across both Post Office and Post Office Insurance
employees was c. 91%. Line Managers and HR Directors receive regular reports of
those who have not completed training for escalation and intervention.

Regular communications are delivered via e-mail and One to relevant audiences to
raise awareness of reporting requirements.

Telco
FIN11 issue and Telecoms Controls

Colin Stuart provided an update on the additional financial controls introduced following from the FIN11
error. He explained that PO Landline and Broadband was provided through a group of suppliers with were
brought together by Fujitsu as a service integrator. There were three financial streams, of which FIN11
related to accruals and payments. An error had been discovered in the accrual report. Fujitsu had
corrected this and PwC had undertaken an independent review of the causes of error, the work required
to resolve the issue, and the reconciliations. PwC had also looked at the FIN15 financial stream.

The error had occurred because there had been a mismatch between the reports run from the Fujitsu
warehouse and the billing system. The over accrual had built up over time. The reconciliations and data
had been corrected. PwC had found no other errors, excepting a small number of minor anomalies with a
low value level.

Anumber of points were raised, including:

whether CS and MS had confidence in the control findings and work done since the identification of
the error and whether the business now worked better? It was reported that the overstatement had
been 1% of revenue. Twice monthly financial interlock conversations were now taking place to test
the trends we were seeing. We now had vi ity of aged accruals? and that report would be moved
into business as usual reporting. The fundamental problem was receiving data from a data warehouse
which introduced an intermediation risk. That risk was being mitigated currently through a higher level
of scrutiny and we also had greater understanding of the risk. Ideally we would get our data from the
source and our current contract with Fujitsu ended in August 2020. We were buying tier 2 billing
systems but there was a risk that the cost of getting data from the source, as was normal for tier 1
billing systems, would turn out to be prohibitive. In that circumstance we would carry our current
intermediation risk into a new contract. The ability to supply data from source would be factored into
the procurement process. The only action not fully completed from the PwC review was the analysis
of aged accruals reporting but as noted this would be moving into BAU reporting. The IA report had
found that balance sheet reconciliations had improved

‘© whether the improved controls in Telco had been extrapolated across the business? It was reported
that there was more verification of finance reviews and standard reporting templates were being used.
Quarterly business reviews took place with the CFOO and the central Finance Team as well as more
testing and review at a business unit level. Grant Thornton was reviewing reliance on third party data.
This review was due to be completed by year end

© whether we were looking at the risk of the security of the systems of third party suppliers? It was
reported that we had a security framework and were testing this. It was suggested that it would be
beneficial to apply this from a contract perspective. CH agreed to consider this further and apply some
systems thinking by looking at a system end to end and how strong it was.

Back Office Transformation (BOT) ~ Legacy Risks

Russell Hancock provided an update on the BOT programme. He reported that there had been delays to.
the launch of the programme and moving to the new system. Timelines had become compressed and we
knew that training staff to use the new system and then put this into practice would be challenging. Post
launch, different systems were not interacting as we had expected them to and we had made some errors.
We had since installed cash centre detectors, who had been early adopters of the system, to help identify
and rectify system errors, Reconciliation errors had reduced to 27 last week. In addition, Transtrack had
deployed their best staff on the project and users were better able to deal with system issues. We had
identified super users and would maintain this group to assist other users.

A significant volume of work remained to be done. The wave 1 solution had been managing reconciliations
via spreadsheets. We now had a data solution that was more robust. Planned order messaging had gone
live two weeks ago and we were managing the risk of excess cash being in branches better through the
Power Bl app. The system still required manual interventions which was sub-optimal.

Compliance
Compliance Report

Jonathan Hill reported that complaints for Telco on landline and fixed broadband were now below the
average for the sector. We had a person dedicated to assessing the root cause of each complaint and the
complaints handling system had improved significantly over the last couple of years. We were also
aligning plans with our suppliers.

The regulator's response to the Competition and Markets Authorities’ findings in relation to the super
complaint was awaited and we did not know yet when their consultation would be issued

The Payment Systems Regulator (PSR) had approached us last year and now wanted a further conversation
on how our systems operated and how we supplied cash around the network. There was a drive to
increase the regulation of PO Limited’s cash supply across the network. However, we were not a
payments system and the PSR would need Government consent to include PO Limited under their
regulations. We were careful about describing what we did and did not want to be considered as a
scheme.

carried out travel money transactions met the Fit and Proper requirements. The Fit and Proper
programme was focussed on being able to meet the June HMRC deadline of providing data that would
allow them to check our verifications. We were also considering options for annual returns and changes.

All agents and assistants should have been vetted at appointment but the HMRC regulations in relation to
travel money transactions went beyond this and required knowledge of the products being sold,
demonstrated through training undertaken. Where we could not provide evidence of compliance with

PCI Compliance

Liz Robson provided an update on PCI Compliance. There were four work streams. The IT work stream
was currently rated red. This was because while the pin pad devices to which we were applying point-to-
point were supported for a reasonable period of time we had discovered that the current estate was only
accredited until next Spring. We were seeking to align accreditation with the support timeline, working
with Global Payments and INGENICO. It was reported that we had engaged DMW, a PCI specialist, to look
at everything that we were going to need as we moved to the cloud. The IT team was working through the
DMW report.

The PCI Compliance programme carried time and risk costs. Considerable attention was being focussed
on this work but the scope of work had grown and we wanted to be sure that we had a full picture of the
scope as soon as possible. It was unlikely that we would achieve full compliance by the end of the financial
year because PCI compliance was binary and required all pin pad devices in every branch to be compliant.
Work was already underway on point-to-point encryption of the pin pads. Communications had alerted
partners to our non-compliance and progress briefings continued through relationship managers. Barclays
had required a much higher level of information than other clients. We had produced a prioritised work
plan and shared this under a Non-Disclosure Agreement (NDA) with Barclays. We were liaising closely with
Global Payments.

Work streams were underway on remediation, PCI standards and on auditing. It was reported that PO
Limited was now a member of the British Retail Consortium (BRC) and that as retailers bore the increasing
cost of PCI compliance it could be a useful issue to raise with BRC.

PZBP

It was reported that IRM had been involved in pen testing and had been able to breach some PZBP devices.
This testing was part of the PCI remediation work stream. An incident report had been raised and daily
meetings were being held with PZBP to work through a priority order of activities. 201 transactions had
been identified as potentials for fraudulent activity but we were analysing whether there had been any
fraudulent activity in practice. We were looking at how we could secure these devices and working on a
remediation plan. We did not believe there had been any breach at present and there was no impact on
the PO Limited network. We were looking at moving PZBP transactions to the Security Operations Centre
(SOC), the Legal team was exploring contractual remedy, and Andrew Goddard, the MD of PZBP, was.
considering compliance funding requirements for the longer term.

Risk Report

Author: Jenny Ellwood Sponsor: Jane MacLeod Meeting date: 25 March 2019

Executive Summary

Context

This paper provides an update on the key risks the Post Office is managing. The
Central Risk team have also been reviewing the risk appetite statements and
developing a plan on next steps.

Questions this paper addresses

What are the key risks facing the business and what is being done to address
these?

What is the latest on risk exceptions and incidents?

What are the emerging risks we face in both the short and medium term and
what are we doing to address these?

What is the latest position on Brexit and required next steps?

What is the status of the Change Portfolio, its current top portfolio risks,
delivery status and key delivery challenges?

Conclusion

The paper reports 4 key risks: PCI, Information Security, Brexit, Change
Workforce. It also provides an update on the Back Office Transformation
Programme.

Additionally following receipt of the judgement of the first trial work is under
way to assess the impact, risk profile and the changes required.

In terms of emerging risks and themes work is continuing to identify ways to
improve the identification and management of any risks the change may
bring to the existing businesses. We are also maintain a watching brief on
the Loyalty Penalty Super Complaint and the risks this may create from a
pricing and product perspective.

An update on the performance of the Change Portfolio is provided of the Gold
and Platinum programmes. The number of programmes reporting a red
status has increased this reporting period. However, the view of the Central
Portfolio team is that overall performance is good.

The Change Excellence programme continues with the design and
implementation of portfolio improvements and only one key risk is called out
for information this month.

Input Sought
There are no decisions required at this time. The Committee is requested to note
this paper.

The Report

What are the key risks facing the business and what is being done to
address these?

i.

There have been few changes to the heatmap status confirmed in January
(see Page 4). However, it has been enhanced to show both this reporting
period status and the previous one following a request from ARC. For red
status, we have also incorporated a date on where we anticipate being back
within appetite. Additionally, a principal risk on third parties has been
incorporated and will be populated ahead of the next ARC. The judgment
of the first trial is under review and the risk profile under both operational
and legal and regulatory is likely to increase.

Appendix 2 shows the latest positon of the top PO Risks; this is held in the
reading room for ease. There has been reduction in the Safety Compliance
risk (Risk 13), which was formerly a red risk. This has been reduced to amber
to reflect improvements in compliance. (Risk 9) regarding the risk of adverse
outcome from the current Group Litigation needs to be reconsidered following
receipt of the judgment of the first trial. It is currently scored as (5:2). The
Key Person Dependency Risk (Risk 11) has been scored as Amber in the last
three quarters. This could be removed from the Top PO risks.

As indicated by the heatmap the principal risk categories which are reported
red and where focus is continuing are under Legal and Regulatory and
Strategic (as per January's report).

Within Legal and Regulatory, PCI remains a key area of focus. The impact
and probability score have remained the same at 16 (4:4). Although
momentum is increasing, scope of our PCI environment is not yet fully
defined. It must also be noted that the Data Discovery element has not
completed and a new date of 31/3/19 has been suggested. DMW (external
consultants) have been brought on board to perform a “root and branch”
assessment of the IT work stream. The headline view is that our PCI scope
has grown and this in turn will drive an increased cost and longer time to
deliver. As such, programme delivery confidence is no more than 50%.

Additionally, IT Security’s 19/20 Security Improvement Plan is in flight.
Current activities to secure Post Office data, such as the implementation of
DLP, Multifactor Authentication, ‘Phishing’ Campaigns and Password strength
work streams are on track.

Within the Strategic category Brexit remains a red risk, although by the date
of the committee we may be clearer on either an agreement on a deal, but
more likely the terms of any extension approved by the EU. Work continues
to mitigate the impact to the Post Office and a full update is shown in
Paragraphs 15-17.

Following a request from ARC in January 2019 to consider whether we have
positioned the Financial Crime Risks correctly, the Central Risk Team
alongside the Financial Crime team will be chairing Workshops with the

What is the latest on risk exceptions and incidents?

8.

10.

11.

Four risk exception requests have been approved since January RCC, all
relating to IT change activity. The aggregate risk score ranges from low
amber to green reflecting the low likelihood of impact on PO as risks are
anticipated to be returned to within appetite by the latest April. Some have
already been closed.

In two cases the request was to accept minor IT security vulnerabilities in
order to prevent delay to the launch of a Branch Hub pilot. A third exception
relates to system field changes made by MoneyGram which could not be fully
tested prior to it going live. This was immediately tested in model office and
no issues were identified. The fourth related to a potential data security
vulnerabilities introduced by not performing pen-testing prior to the Azure
data migration. The pen test was carried out and some vulnerabilities were
identified and are now under review.

The Central Risk Team also worked with the business to ascertain whether a
risk exception was needed to allow the use of temporary IDs for WH Smith
staff required to work in newly franchised branches. WH Smith flagged they
had been unable to get staff fully vetted due to delays in being unable to
recruit. Activity is planned to raise awareness of the risk exceptions process
and to encourage early engagement with the Central Risk Team.

The number of significant risk incidents reported over the last two months
has remained at similar levels to the previous period. The two most common
incidents reported are data breaches /potential breaches, and gas attacks on
ATMs. An update on the data incidents is covered within the Compliance
Paper.

What is the latest position on Brexit and required next steps?

15.

16.

17.

Brexit ‘No Deal’ Contingency plans continue. An Operations Working Group
is in place to ensure we have sufficient communications and training for
branch colleagues. A call has also been made for management to be on
standby for two days branch support during the 25th March and 5th April.
Retail have identified the branches which may require more support.

Additionally, following further guidance from BEIS on Data, and a call Paula
and Rob Houghton had with the MP Richard Harrington, we have expanded
the list of third parties we need to write to. Contacting those that are data
processors and who may be processing the data in an EU Country. So far no
new issues have been identified.

Supply Chain have if required contingency plans they can deploy opening all
depots on the weekends of 23rd and 30th March to provide additional
services. Three reserve vehicles can also be in Portsmouth depot and teams
can be deployed should assist Kent and East Sussex areas should traffic be
heavy.

What is the status of the Change Portfolio, its current top portfolio risks, delivery
status and key delivery challenges?

18.

19.

20.

21.

22.

We have established a Change Risk Management Group (CRMG) who set the
change risk strategy and policy. Its purpose is provide a proportionate,
comprehensive and dynamic approach to the management of change risks
across the Post Office.

CRMG is responsible for advising the Risk Director and the Strategic Portfolio
Office Director on all aspects of Post Office change risk management and
making appropriate recommendations to them on all significant matters
relating to the Post Office’s change risk strategy. It ensures all key change
portfolio risks are ranked, responded to, reported on and reviewed. It
ensures such risks have regard to the views of the Investment Committee
and agrees those change portfolio risks (as well as any significant
programme/project risks) which should be escalated to the RCC and ARC.

The overall status of the portfolio remains Amber. This is driven primarily by
the fact that realised benefits for 2018/19 are forecast to be just outside
tolerance (10.4% reduction) but looking to be recovered by the 3 year
position. Investment for 2018-19 is amber. Work continues to improve the
links between Project activities and deliverables and the benefits to be
achieved.

2019/20 portfolio prioritisation has been completed. It delivers the goals of
the 3 year plan. An assessment against delivery constraints, which exist in
our internal and external supply chain, is in progress. However, the
judgement of the first trial, may need us to reconsider our priorities and
focus.

Key risks are around increasing scope, mapping benefits to scope and the
overall capability of the resource pool against the need of the portfolio (see

Programme updates - Back Office Transformation

27.

28.

At the time of go-live manual workarounds had been put in place to support
6 business processes and 1 system fix had been implemented. The business
regarded the Supply Chain manual interventions as manageable but
acknowledged that, at that point, the cash reconciliation process had not yet
been undertaken.

Since then a number of issues are being proactively managed. These
include:

e Forecasting: the business have been using standard cash estimates for
Branches which as expected led to increased cash holdings of around
£50m across the network. An interim forecasting solution was planned
for the beginning of March but has been slightly delayed. Branches are
now reverting to pre-announced sterling values from standard
estimates. This will prevent further increase in cash holdings. From a
business risk perspective we agreed prior to going live that we could
operate with standards for 3 months, and this level of cash in the
network remains well under previous normal levels.

e Reconciliations: We expected the Transtrack CWC to CFS financial
reconciliation problem to deteriorate at go-live as volumes increased and
new processes came on line. What was unexpected was an inability to
fully reconcile the position between systems thereby explaining all
variations. Although the project can now identify every missing
transaction in Transtrack CWC further works is ongoing to identify all
root causes. Over the coming 6-12 weeks the Transtrack development
team are delivering fixes against a priority schedule every 2 weeks to
resolve this problem.

Appendix 1. Post Office Principal Risks Definition

‘The risk of failed transaction processes or process management that are “externally facing”
provide a service for external customers, including agents, clients.

Failure to successfully design, market, deliver, support and improve our product portfolio in a
‘manner that meets customer needs and delivers desired customer outcomes, may lead to adverse
financial and reputational impact.

The risk that Post Office is unable to manage effectively the payment process. Failure to comply
with contract regulation, manage high volume of payments or record incorrect payments may result
in a reputation damage and financial loss.

Inadequate or insufficient processes and systems for creating, maintaining, retrieving and disposing
of contractual, commercial, legal and intellectual property related documents and records, in line
with legal and business policy requirements.

The potential for failures in a procurement process resulting in loss, disruption, penalties or
reputation damage. The management of the procurement channels is not optimal (reduced
purchase options), and therefore could affect the continuity of the business and expose the
business to incur high costs that could therefore reduce margins of profitability.

The risk to health and safety of colleagues, contractors, customers and third parties (in the
workplace, branches and also affected by business activities e.g. acquisition of a vehicle. Breach of
Health and Safety regulations).

Failure to ensure the physical safety of employees and agents in response to incidents such as theft,
robbery, burglary, terrorism and site safety, could expose Post Office to significant costs for
reimbursement for damages and remediation, operational disruption and reputational damage.
The risk or instances of interruptions (including critical sites, critical and core infrastructure,
suppliers and colleagues), as a consequence of internal or external events due to a lack of defined,
tested and effective continuity plans and recovery strategies, which may lead to financial,
‘operational and reputational damage.

The risk of losing the trusted brand image of Post Office that prevents the business from
successfully operating in its chosen markets and/or achieving its North Star ambitions, The
‘organisation may lose market share, key resources or its ability to compete because of the
perception of its behaviour with customers, agents, suppliers and partners.

‘The risk that business operations do not meet customer expectations due to a reduced focus on
their satisfaction (e.g, vulnerable customers).

Inadequate procedures around the management of IT security resulting in cyber threat (denial of
service, theft/unauthorised disclosure of valuable information) and insider threat
(theft/unauthorised disclosure of valuable information, accidental disclosure, and illegal software
Use), and leading to financial loss, service disruption and reputational damage.

The risk or instances of interruptions to IT infrastructure and systems (including at IT suppliers) as a
consequence of internal or extemal events and due to inadequate recovery strategies and/or
continuity of systems and controls.

Inappropriate, inadequate or unavailable technological infrastructure (e.g. HW, SW) to support
current and future business needs in an efficient and effective way.

The risk that Post Office is unable to effectively manage:

- dependency on 3rd parties to deliver, support and improve services for which they have been.
contracted to the required time, cost or quality; and

= its contracts, including failure to negotiate the favourable commercial terms and conditions in
contracts, ensuring compliance with the terms and conditions. documenting and agreeing any
changes or amendments that may arise during its implementation or execution, which may have an
impact on financial and operational performance.

The risk of acts that create financial loss, and/or are intended to defraud, misappropriate property
or circurnvent the law.

The risk of financial crime (money laundering, terrorist financing, bribery and corruption, fraud, etc)
resulting from inadequate or failed internal processes, people and systems, or from external events,
leading to regulatory breaches and and/or reputational damage to the Post Office; and financial
and commercial loss to the Post Office and its customers, colleagues and clients.

Inadequate procedures around the management of information and its security results in a breach
of valuable information (customer, colleague, commercial and/or operational) leading to financial
loss and reputational damage.

‘The risk that Post Office faces exposure to losses, penalties and/or reputation damage when it fails
to act in accordance with legal and regulatory obligations.

The risk that legal action will be taken because of an individual's or organisation's actions, inaction,
products, services or other events. Managers and employees, individually or in collusion, commit
illegal acts, exposing the organisation, the management itself and the employees to their
consequences.

Principal Risks Risk Definition

The risk that the administration of tax/VAT may be more costly - or otherwise more burdensome
than foreseen. The risk may arse from existing tax/VAT laws and practice, or from changes in
tax/VAT laws and practice.

‘making and the control mechanisms to ensure strategies and management instructions are
implemented effectively

reputational loss, from inappropriate or poor customer treatment or business conduct.

‘The risk that Post Office suffers reputational damage, loss of shareholder confidence and /or
financial loss from the adoption of inappropriate accounting polices, ineffective controls over
financial reporting, failure to manage the associated risks in changes to tax, law and failure to
disclose accurate and timely information.

operational needs of the business.

performance) could reduce revenue and EBITDAS.

- aimed at establishing and maintaining a competitive advantage, in response to actions adopted
by main competitors, disruptors or new operators; and

- in response to changing markets (including demographic, technological and other external market
events) and customers changing service needs and expectations.

The risk that Post office does not have a Strategic Plan or deliver its North Star strategic priorities

respond to changes in the external and internal environment.

Inadequate, inappropriate or ineffective processes relating to recruitment, development,

financial, operational or reputational impact.

Inadequate and/or inappropriate capabilites, processes, organisation, data and culture to identity
prioritise, design, deliver and manage the portfolio of change activities resulting in delays, cost
overruns, and non-realisation of benefits and in turn leading to the business not being able to
operate the desire capabilities, processes and systems to support North Star ambitions.

Report

Key issues

Telecoms Compliance

Text Relay

4

5

In the last report it was noted that we planned to self-report the Text Relay
issue to Ofcom. This was done on 7‘ January. We responded to Ofcom's
questions on 21°t February. On 14' March we were informed by Ofcom that
it will be opening a formal investigation into our compliance with General
Conditions related to Text Relay.

Ofcom has decided on this action due to the nature and length of time of
the breach rather than its size, while acknowledging our cooperative
approach to date, the new controls we have in place and our plans to refund
customers. The notification has now been placed on the Ofcom Website, as
a Consumer Enforcement Bulletin.

A formal investigation team has been established by Ofcom with an
expectation to aim to reach a provisional view on the next steps in the case by
July. We are establishing our own team to support the investigation (to
include the Telecoms team, Compliance, Legal and Comms). Ofcom has
stated it would be open in principle to a settlement on this case, and we are
reviewing this approach.

As advised to Ofcom we are in the process of compensating customers and
will report back at the next RCC the outcome of how many customers we
have been able to issue the refund to.

We expect to receive closer regulatory scrutiny in the coming year.

Complaints

6

Post Office appeared in Ofcom’s broadband complaints table for the first
time in January and were under the industry average. Post Office is also at
our lowest level in fixed line, again under the industry average. Ofcom’s
complaints enforcement team is happy with our progress. Work still

continues on signposting complaints to ensure we are picking these up

Ofcom will publish its Comparing Service Quality Report at the end of April
2019. It is unclear yet whether we will appear in both broadband and fixed
line sections. Work is underway on the $137A data request we received
from Ofcom, which means we have to implement significant system changes
in time to supply Ofcom with data for their report next year. Failure to
implement the changes accurately or on time is subject to a fine.

Early Termination Charges (“ETCs”)

8

10

11

Following Ofcom’s scrutiny of ETCs (and fines applied to large telecoms
firms) we received a letter in January 2019 reminding Post Office of its ETC
transparency obligations on our website. We formally replied to Ofcom and
met with its enforcement team, setting out the changes we are making to
our web pages. Ofcom understood we were in the midst of changing the
website and was satisfied with the new layout.

Also, informally Ofcom asked if we were changing our ETCs. We explained
that we were reviewing our current ETC charging.

We do now expect to change our ETCs slightly in the coming months to
ensure we are charging customers in line with the current sales channel mix
and average tenure and to continue to ensure we are transparent and easy
to understand. This had not been reviewed for a number of years and we
do not have the historical business justification for the old charges, which
may be challenged. The exposure to penalties from Ofcom has been
assessed by our legal and business teams as low for two key reasons:

a. We have not been carrying out the sorts of charging practices (e.g.,
ignoring monthly discounts as part of ETC calculations) that caused EE
and Virgin Media to get fined;

b. Changes in our ETCs will only increase or decrease by a small amount
across our products and we have the same price across a customer's
contract period.

Additionally, since early 2018, we have been trialling waiving ETCs if a
customer challenges the charges in order to help improve customer
satisfaction.

Information Protection & Assurance

12

What is the general forward-looking regulatory agenda?

Citizens’ Advice (CA) Super Complaint to Competition and Markets Authority
(CMA) relating to the ‘loyalty penalty’

a

45

CA raised concerns in November 2018 about long term customers paying
more for goods and services, which it refers to as ‘the loyalty penalty’. CA
had identified five key markets where it has concerns about the loyalty
penalty: broadband, mortgages, cash savings, insurance, and mobile.

Excluding mobile, all of these services are offered through Post Office
channels. The CMA has strongly supported the CA complaint and is pursuing
actions via the different regulators. To a large extent this is already
supporting the direction of travel of the regulators Ofcom and FCA who are
following a more interventionist agenda in their regulatory approach to the
‘loyalty penalty’ including price controls.

POST OFFICE PAGE 1 OF 7
ARC NOTING PAPER

The Finii issue and Telecoms Controls

Author: Colin Stuart Meeting date: 25th March 2019

Executive Summary

Context

Post Office’s Telecoms business provides broadband and landline services to customers
through a selection of providers, managed by Fujitsu as the overall service integrator.

Post Office uses reports generated by Fujitsu (known as Fin02; Fini1; and Fin15) to
account for revenue. In May 2018 Post Office identified an error in the Fin11 report
which had the impact of overstating income by £5.2m in 2017/18.

Fujitsu corrected and restated Fin11 and PwC conducted an independent review which
established the cause of the error, reviewed the correctional work, conducted
reconciliations and reviewed the business logic behind the generation of the Fin11 and
Fin15 reports. A satisfactory internal review of the Fin02 report had previously been
carried out.

This paper sets out the causes of the Fin11 issue, its resolution, the conclusions drawn,
and the actions taken to strengthen controls in Telecoms and in the wider Post Office.

Questions addressed in this report
1. What was the Telecoms Fin11 issue and how did it arise?

2. How was the Fin11 issue resolved, what were the conclusions and what are the
recommended actions to prevent a recurrence?

3. How well does the improved Telecoms control environment manage the risks?
4. What are the potential wider control issues and how have these been addressed?

Conclusion

The Fin11 issue arose from a mismatch between the billing system and the Fujitsu data
warehouse which resulted in records being incorrectly treated as live in the Fin11 report,
but correctly treated as on-hold by the billing system.

The issue was resolved by Fujitsu correcting the Fin11 report and refreshing the data
warehouse from the billing system. An external independent review by PwC validated
and reconciled the Fin11 and Fin15 reports, confirmed the business logic behind them
and verified that there were no other significant errors. A list of actions was prepared
to address the control environment in Telecoms and the wider business.

Good progress has been made on the actions, with the majority implemented and the
remainder underway. The improved Telecoms control environment manages the risks,
but the Fujitsu data management and reporting structures mean that risks remain and
these require high levels of verification and review type controls in mitigation. In
September 2018 this improved control environment identified a smaller (£0.4m) issue
with Fin11 relating to old New Call records. The arrangement with Fujitsu is being
retendered in 2020 and clear requirements for data management, controls and
reporting will be included as part of the specification for that.

Input sought

The ARC is asked to note the contents of this report, questioning and challenging as
appropriate.

Strictly Confidential The Fin11 issue and Telecoms Controls

Strictly Confidential The Fin11 issue and Telecoms Controls

What was the Telecoms Fin11 issue and how did it arise?

. Post Office’s Telecoms business provides broadband and landline services to
customers via a selection of technology and service providers, managed by Fujitsu
who act as the overall system integrator to provide a packed service. The business
has revenues of c£155m and direct contribution of cE30m

Fujitsu generates three reports which Post Office uses to account for revenue:
¢ Fin02 - which details customer balances
¢ Fin11 - which calculates deferred and accrued revenue
e Fin15 - which provides summaries of invoiced net value

In May 2018, Post Office reviews identified an error in the generation of the Fin11
report. The issue was caused by the billing system (operated by MDS) correctly
marking records relating to the Line Rental Saver promotion as on-hold, but that
status not being recorded in the Fujitsu data warehouse. The Fin11 report uses
that data warehouse as its source of information. As a result, Fin11 incorrectly
accrued income in respect of c117,000 records over a 15 month period, resulting
in an overstatement of revenue in Post Office’s books totalling £5.2m.

How was the Fin11 issue resolved, what are the conclusions and what
are the recommendations to prevent a recurrence?

. The issue was resolved by Fujitsu correcting the Fin11 report and refreshing the
data warehouse from the MDS billing system.

Independent external reviews by PwC of Fin11 and Fin15 were completed in July
(Fin11) and August 2018 (Fin15). These validated and reconciled the Fin11 and
Fin15 reports, confirmed the business logic behind them and verified that there
were no other significant errors.

The PwC work also considered why the error was not discovered earlier. The
conclusions were:

e Reliance was placed on the incorrect assumption that the Fin11 report
produced by Fujitsu was an accurate reflection of the data in the underlying
billing system.

« The level of rigour and scepticism applied to understanding the reasons for the
increase in the Line Rental revenue accrual was insufficient given the trend in
the balance and the size of the size of the error.

e There was a lack of clarity/understanding of the contract with Fujitsu with
regard to the roles and responsibilities (at both Post Office and Fujitsu) for
ensuring the accuracy of data held within Fujitsu’s data warehouse and how
the billing system data was used to populate the data warehouse.

* Documentation of the monthly balance sheet review did not have sufficient
factual support to validate the comments made. Process documentation to
ensure the robust and consistent application of the review was not in place.

. PwC prepared a list of Telco specific recommendations to address the weakness in
the control environment:

« Ageing assessment: Identification and scrutiny of the Line Rental accrued
revenue balance would have been facilitated by an effective aging analysis. It
was recommended that Post Office work with Fujitsu to create a report.

Post Office Limited - Audit Risk & Compliance Committee-25/03/19

POL00423454

2.5

3.

3.25

3.3.

3.4.

« Independent checks: Post Office should further develop the process of periodic
review of the information within the Fin11 versus other trends and balances.

e Impact on other reports: As PwC’s work focused primarily on the level of
accrued revenue, they also recommended that any other potentially
misleading statements within the regular monthly reporting commentary
should be identified and considered.

« Supplementary Reporting: PwC noted that they were made aware of additional
“drill down” reports that are available but those reports did not appear to be
in regular use. The additional level of detail contained therein may have
assisted in the more timely discovery of the issue.

« Risk Assessment: Post Office should develop formal guidance to support the
risk assessment that takes place as part of the monthly balance sheet review.
This would assist in a more rigorous and consistent approach.

« Data Retention: Post Office should insist on a level of data retention from its
suppliers to ensure that any future errors are identified and preserved to allow
any re-performance where necessary.

« Data integrity: POL should consider the contractual responsibility for
confirming the accuracy of the data within the Fujitsu data warehouse.

The actions developed to address these recommendations and their current
status are set out in Appendix 1.

. Good progress has been made in implementing PwC’s recommendations. The key
outstanding point is the aged analysis reporting from Fujitsu which has taken time
in the scoping and definition phases, and in finding a build slot. A draft report is
available, has successfully passed testing and is moving into production, with
acceptance anticipated in March 2019 for use at the 2018/19 year end.

How well does the improved Telecoms control environment manage
the risks?

. The improved control environment and regular formal interlock between Telecoms
and Finance have increased our ability to manage the risks of the Telecoms
business.

In addition, the general level of scrutiny applied by the Telecoms and Finance team
has increased significantly, with detailed joint review of weekly data. This has
identified small items for correction/follow-up with Fujitsu that might previously
have grown unnoticed.

However, there are inherent risks with the interface between the Fujitsu Data
Warehouse (that is used as source for our financial reporting) and the MDS billing
systems. The ideal would be for reports to be directly derived from and
automatically reconciled to the billing system. However, the Fujitsu contract is
being re-tendered in 2020 and so large scale changes would be expensive and
potentially short term. We will ensure that our requirements for robust reporting,
data integrity and a strong control environment are incorporated in to the re-
tender documentation. In the meantime we will continue to mitigate the risks
using the higher levels of scrutiny described.

In September 2018 the greater scrutiny and stronger controls between Post Office
and Fujitsu highlighted a data issue within the Fujitsu data warehouse. The impact
was relatively small at £0.35m and occurred in relation to the transfer of customers
in the New Call acquisition in 2017:

« In between the data extract and the actual transfer date around 1,000 New
Call customers indicated that they did not want to transfer.

e Steps were taken at the time to ensure that the accounts were never live on

e However, the corresponding records in the Fujitsu data warehouse were not

any more work.

error. We propose to rebuild this in Q1 and are in the process of bringing in a
specialist contractor to assist.

4. What are the potential wider control issues and how have these been

4.2.

4.3.

addressed?
4.1.

The Fin11 issue highlighted some areas of potential improvement across the wider
business:

« Overreliance by Post Office on third party data for revenue recognition

« Inadequacy of processes and controls in place at third parties

« Lack of understanding by Post Office business owners over processes driving
revenue recognition

e Lack of existence and robustness of controls and reviews around third party
data

Previously, the focus in the Post Office control environment was on granularity of
controls - aiming to mitigate all risks, big and small, through detailed controls.

Prompted by the Fin11 work, changes have been made over the past 6 months
which involved creating a more rounded control environment. This has included
establishing an appropriate level of detective and preventative controls for key
risks:

* Good quality balance sheet reconciliations — the old “probity” process was
inadequate. The new process focuses on the user of the reconciliations i.e.
what information would someone require to identify an issue in the balance.
Training has been given across the business and new reconciliation templates
have been implemented.

« Supportable analysis - balance sheet reviews should be less discursive, instead
focussing on agreeing items back to support and performing analytical and
ageing reviews. These are now performed routinely, with quarterly deep dives.

e Appropriate challenge of management - regular formal meetings with
management to understand and challenge trading, balance sheet trends and
balances in the context of business logic and performance. These are in place.

« Review of detailed controls - detailed controls have been reviewed for naivety;
irrelevant controls have been removed; controls have been re-worked to
ensure a risk focused approach; and a detailed review of processes linked to

POST OFFICE LIMITED PAGE 1 OF 4
AUDIT, RISK AND COMPLIANCE COMMITTEE

Cyber Security Update

Authors: David Meldrum/Mick Mitchell Sponsor: Rob Houghton Meeting date: 25 March 2019

Executive Summary

Context

This report provides a short update on the progress with the Security Strategy that was
shared with ARC in January 2019, and an update on the management of recent security
incidents that have occurred.

Questions this paper addresses

.
.

What progress have we made on implementing the Security strategy?

What is the progress of the implementation of Archer?

What remedial activities have we carried out as a result of the recent security
incidents that have occurred?

Conclusion

1.

We continue to make progress with our Security Strategy. We have prioritised
our investment around 3 key areas — improving the reach and capability of the
SOC, including improving our Computer Security Incident Response Team
(CSIRT); focusing on improving the management around Data Security including
Data Leakage Prevention (DLP); and improving the management and assurance
of compliance of our 3" parties. Solid progress has been made in these 3 areas
already (see below) and an investment case will be presented for further funding
in March. Furthermore, the current Deloitte review of Cyber and Information
Security seems to validate the priorities highlighted in the Security Strategy.

Whilst RSA Archer has been implemented for the Security Operations Centre for
managing security incidents, the rollout of the additional risk modules including
third party assurance has not been agreed or prioritised at present. Further work
is required with the Risk function on the best way forward.

Since December 2018, we have been the subject of 3 security incidents which
have been actively managed. In addition, we have carried a pre-emptive phishing
exercise across the organisation to raise awareness of the dangers of email
phishing attacks. All of the above events have further validated the priorities we
have set for our security strategy, especially around the need to improve our SOC
and CSIRT capability.

Input Sought

The ARC is requested to note the progress made, and provide feedback on the report.
